Ijarah — Hire and Lease
intermediate
8 min
Liability for Misuse of Hired Animal in Ijarah
Commentary on Al-Hidayah explaining hirer's liability under ijarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings on liability when a hirer deviates from the agreed usage of a hired animal, and apply the conditions for full or proportional liability.

Core Idea
Deviation from agreed load type or weight creates liability only if the change increases harm; proportional liability applies unless the load is unbearable.

In Ijarah (hire) of animals, the contract specifies the type and weight of load. If the hirer loads something else, the liability for damage depends on whether the new load is similar, lighter, or more harmful. This lesson from the commentary on Al-Hidayah explains the three scenarios: permissible variation, extra rider, and overloading. Understanding these rules is essential for determining when the hirer becomes a dhamin (liable for compensation).

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope of permission (ma'dhun fih)
Determine what the contract authorizes: type and weight of load or number of riders. The hirer is only permitted to use the animal within that scope.
Permission is defined by the owner's consent, explicit or implied by custom.
2
Distinguish permissible vs. impermissible variations in load type
If the specified goods are replaced with something similar (e.g., barley for wheat) or lighter (e.g., cotton), it is permissible. If more harmful (e.g., iron or salt), it is not.
Similarity is judged by the degree of harm to the animal; lighter goods are less harmful, heavier or concentrated loads are more.
3
Determine liability for extra rider
If the hirer takes an additional rider without permission and the animal dies, he is liable for half its value. Weight is not considered; instead, the number of riders matters due to the impossibility of weighing a person and the variable skill of riders.
The analogy is to the count of criminals in injuries (jinayat).
4
Apply proportional liability for overloading
If the hirer loads more than the agreed weight of the same type (e.g., extra wheat), liability is proportional to the excess weight. The cause of death is divided between authorized and unauthorized parts.
Proportional liability applies only if the total load is within the animal's normal capacity.
5
Identify when full liability applies
If the overload is so heavy that the animal cannot normally carry it, the hirer is fully liable for the entire value, as there is no permission at all (beyond custom).
Custom (urf) determines the normal capacity; anything beyond is a clear transgression.

اشرف الهدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ۱۲ جیسی ہو ضرر میں اس سے کم ہو۔ جیسے جو اور تل ، کیونکہ یہ اجازت کے تحت میں داخل ہے تفاوت نہ ہونے یا اول سے بہتر ہونے کی وجہ سے اور اس کو یہ اختیار نہیں کہ لادے ایسی چیز جو گیہوں سے زیادہ مضر ہو۔ جیسے نمک اور لوہا، ان کی رضا مندی نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے۔ اگر کرایہ پر لیا جانور اس لئے کہ اس پر روئی لا دے گا جس کا وزن بیان کر دیا تو اس کو یہ اختیار نہیں کہ اس کے ہم وزن لوہا لا د لے ۔ کیونکہ بسا اوقات لو ہا اس کو زیادہ مضر ہوتا ہے۔ اس لئے کہ لوہا مجمع ہو جاتا ہے اس کی پیٹھ پر ایک ہی جگہ اور روئی اس کی پیٹھ پر پھیل جاتی ہے۔ اگر کرایہ پر لیا چوپایہ سواری کے لئے اور اپنے پیچھے دوسرے کو سوار کر لیا اور وہ ہلاک ہو گیا تو اس کی نصف قیمت کا ضامن ہوگا اور بوجھ کا اعتبار نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ کبھی ہلکے سوار کی نادانی چوپایہ کو خستہ کر دیتی ہے اور کبھی بھاری آدمی کی سواری اس پر خفیف ہوتی ہے اس کی شہسواری جانے کی وجہ سے اور اس لئے کہ آدمی وزنی چیز نہیں ہے تو اس کا وزن پہنچانا غیر ممکن ہے تو سواروں کی گنتی معتبر ہوئی جیسے جنایات میں مجرموں کی گفتی، اگر کرایہ پر لیا گیہوں کی ایک مقدار لادنے کے لئے پھر اس سے زیادہ لادا اور وہ ہلاک ہو گیا تو زائدہ بوجھ کے بقدر ضامن ہوگا۔ کیونکہ وہ ایسے بوجھ سے تلف ہوا جس میں وہ ماذون ہے اور جس میں ماذون نہیں ہے اور سبب یہی بوجھ ہے تو وہ ان دونوں پر تقسیم ہو گا۔ مگر جب کہ اتنا بوجھ ہو کہ اس کو ایسا جانور نہیں اٹھا سکتا تو اس وقت ضامن ہو گا کل قیمت کا کیونکہ اس کی اجازت بالکل نہیں عادت سے باہر ہونے کی وجہ سے۔
تشریح قوله وان سمى نوعاً ... الخ - ایک شخص نے کوئی جانور کرایہ پر لیا اور اس پر جو بو جھلا دے گا اس کی نوع اور مقدار بیان کر دی۔ مثلاً یہ کہ دومن گیہوں لا دے گا۔ تو وہ اس پر گیہوں جیسی اور کوئی چیز مثلاً دو من جو بھی لا دسکتا ہے۔ اسی طرح جو اس سے ہلکی ہو جیسے قتل وغیرہ وہ بھی لا سکتا ہے دومن ، لیکن جو چیز گیہوں سے زیادہ نقصان دہ ہو وہ نہیں لا سکتا جیسے لوہا اور نمک وغیرہ کیونکہ موجر اس سے
راضی نہیں ہے۔
43. Book of Ijarah. Ashraf al-Hidayah, Urdu commentary of Hidayah, Volume 12: such that it is less harmful. Like barley and sesame, because this falls under permission due to no difference or being better than the first. And he does not have the authority to load something more harmful than wheat, like salt and iron, because of lack of consent. If he hired an animal to load cotton whose weight was specified, he does not have the authority to load iron of equal weight, because sometimes iron is more harmful to it, because iron gathers in one place on its back while cotton spreads on its back. If he hired a quadruped for riding and seated another behind him and it died, he is liable for half its price, and weight is not considered, because sometimes the folly of a light rider debilitates the animal and sometimes the riding of a heavy person is light on it due to his horsemanship, and because a person is not a weighty thing, so weighing him is impossible, so the count of riders is considered, like the count of criminals in injuries. If he hired [the animal] to load a certain quantity of wheat, then loaded more and it died, he is liable proportional to the excess load, because it perished from a load in which he was authorized and in which he was not authorized, and the cause is this same load, so it is divided between them. But when the load is such that such an animal cannot carry it, then he is liable for the full price because there is absolutely no permission due to being beyond custom. Commentary on his statement: 'And if he specified a type...': A person hired an animal and specified the type and quantity of load he will put on it, for example, he said 'I will load two maunds of wheat.' Then he can load something similar to wheat, e.g., two maunds of barley, and also something lighter, like cotton and such, he can load two maunds, but he cannot load something more harmful than wheat, like iron and salt, because the owner is not pleased with that.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Al-Hidayah. It addresses the liability of the hirer (musta'jir) when he uses the hired animal in a manner different from what was agreed. The author first discusses the case where the type of goods is specified (e.g., wheat). The hirer may load something similar (like barley) or lighter (like cotton) because they fall under the permission granted. However, he cannot load something more harmful (like salt or iron) because that was not consented to. The text then covers taking an extra rider: if the hirer takes an additional rider and the animal dies, he is liable for half its value, because skill, not weight, is the factor. Next, it addresses overloading: if the hirer loads more than agreed and the animal dies, he is liable proportionally for the excess weight, unless the total load is beyond what the animal could normally carry, in which case he is fully liable. The sub-commentary reinforces the rule: permissible to load similar or lighter goods, but not more harmful ones.

Key Terms
ماذون (ma'dhun): Authorized by the contract; within the scope of permission granted by the owner.
ضامن (dhamin): Liable to pay compensation (damages) for the loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a hirer agrees to load wheat but loads barley of equal weight, and the animal dies, what is the ruling?
A) The hirer is liable for the full value because barley is different.
B) The hirer is not liable because barley is similar in harm.
C) The hirer is liable for half the value.
D) The hirer is liable only for the excess weight.

Key Takeaway
The hirer is liable only when the deviation increases harm beyond the agreed scope; for extra riders, fixed half liability applies; for overloading, liability is proportional unless the load is unbearable.

Hirer's Liability for Customary vs. Non-Customary Actions: The Hanafi Dispute
Presents intra-Hanafi dispute on hirer's liability for ordinary reins-pulling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding hirer's liability for an animal's death caused by beating or reins-pulling, and identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
A beating that is customary is part of the contract; a beating that breaks custom breaks liability.

This lesson unpacks an intra-Hanafi dispute from the chapter of Ijara. When a hired animal dies due to the hirer's actions, two views emerge: one holding the hirer always liable, the other making liability conditional on non-customary behavior. Understanding the reasoning behind each view sharpens your grasp of how conditions are implied in contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What did the hirer do? Beating or pulling reins causing death. State the hukm for each of the two views.
The ruling difference is full liability vs conditional liability.
2
Extract the dalil of Imam Abu Hanifa
What is his proof? Permission conditioned on safety. The beating is unnecessary for ordinary driving; it breaks the condition.
He uses the analogy of walking safely on a public road.
3
Extract the dalil of the Sahibayn
Their proof: customary actions are included in the absolute contract. If the action is mut'āraf, the owner's permission covers it.
They rely on 'urf (custom) to define the scope of permission.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, any beating leads to liability; under Sahibayn, only non-customary beating. Give an example of customary vs non-customary.
E.g., light tapping to urge is customary; severe beating is not.
5
Note the tarjih
Which view is mufta bihi? The fatwa is on Sahibayn's view, and Abu Hanifa himself reportedly reverted.
This is recorded in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Ghaliyat al-Bayan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وان كبح الداية ... الخ - اگر متاجر کے مارنے یا لگام کھینچنے سے سواری ہلاک ہوگئی تو امام صاحب کے نزدیک مستاجر پر کل قیمت کا تاوان آئے گا۔ صاحبین اور ائمہ ثلاثہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر اس نے ایسی مار ماری یا اس طرح لگام کھینچی جو دستور عرف کے خلاف ہے تو تاوان لازم ہوگا ورنہ نہیں۔ کیونکہ جو کام متعارف ہو وہ مطلق عقد کے تحت میں داخل ہوتا ہے تو یہ فعل اس کی اجازت سے پایا گیا لہذا ضامن نہ ہو گا۔
قوله ولابي حنيفة .... الخ - امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مالک کی طرف سے اجازت مقید بشرط سلامتی ہے اور اس نے اس کے خلف کیا ہے کیونکہ ایسی مار مارنے اور لگام کھینچنے کے بغیر چلانا ممکن تھا۔ مارنا اور باگ کھینچنا تو تیز دوڑانے کے لئے ہوتا ہے۔ پس اس میں اجازت شرط سلامتی کے ساتھ مقید ہے جیسے عام راستہ میں چلانا جائز ہے بشرطیکہ سلامتی کے ساتھ ہو۔ یہاں تک کہ اگر کسی کا نقصان کرے تو ضامن ہوگا۔ درمختار میں ہے کہ فتوی صاحبین کے قول پر ہے اور غالیہ البیان میں تمہ کے حوالہ سے منقول ہے کہ امام ابو

ضي الہ نے بھی اسی کی طرف رجوع کر لیا تھا۔
His statement: 'And if he pulled the reins of the mount...' - If the riding animal dies due to the hirer's beating or pulling the reins, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the hirer is liable for the full price. The Sahibayn and the three Imams say: if he beat it or pulled the reins in a way contrary to custom, then liability is incumbent, otherwise not, because a customary action falls under the absolute contract, so this action occurred with permission, therefore he is not liable. His statement: 'And for Abu Hanifa...' - Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that permission from the owner is conditioned upon safety, and he has acted contrary to that, because driving is possible without such beating and pulling. Beating and pulling the reins are only for speeding. Thus, permission is conditioned on safety, like driving on a public road is permissible provided it is safe, to the extent that if it causes harm to someone, he is liable. In al-Durr al-Mukhtar, the fatwa is on the statement of the Sahibayn, and in Ghaliyat al-Bayan, it is narrated from [Imam] that Imam Abu Hanifa himself also reverted to that.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute. It discusses the liability of a hirer when his actions (beating or pulling the reins) cause the hired animal's death. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the hirer is always liable for the full value because permission from the owner is implicitly conditioned upon the safety of the animal. The owner's permission to drive does not extend to actions that could harm the animal. On the other hand, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that if the hirer's actions are customary (mut'āraf) – i.e., what people normally do when driving an animal – then he is not liable, because such actions are covered by the general permission of the contract. Only if he acts in an unusual manner that is against custom is he liable. The passage then notes that later fatwa (as in al-Durr al-Mukhtar) follows the Sahibayn, and some report that even Imam Abu Hanifa eventually adopted their view. The author emphasizes the distinction between explicit and implicit conditions in the contract.

Key Terms
متعارف (mut'āraf): Customary; actions that are normally done by people in that situation.
اجازت مقید بشرط سلامتی (ijāzat muqayyad bi-sharṭ salāmatī): Permission conditioned upon safety; the owner's consent is deemed to require the animal's safety.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, under what condition is the hirer NOT liable for the death of a hired animal due to beating?
A) If the beating was mild.
B) If the beating was customary and according to normal practice.
C) If the animal was old.
D) If the hirer did not intend harm.

Key Takeaway
The liability of a hirer for damage to a hired animal hinges on whether the action was customary; the mufta bihi view follows the Sahibayn, considering custom as part of the contract's implied permission.

The Role of Custom ('Urf) in Shaping Contractual Liability: The Hirer's Beating Case
Presents intra-Hanafi dispute on hirer's liability for ordinary reins-pulling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the principle of 'urf (custom) influences the scope of contractual permission in Hanafi fiqh, using the hirer's liability dispute as an example.

Core Idea
Custom bridges the gap between explicit and implicit contractual terms.

In the previous lesson, we saw the dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on hirer's liability. This lesson zooms into the methodological tool of 'urf (custom) that the Sahibayn used. Understanding how custom defines 'permission' helps you see how Hanafi jurists balance the owner's implicit conditions with common practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the role of 'urf in the Sahibayn's argument
The Sahibayn rule that if the action is mut'āraf (customary), it is included in the absolute contract. Custom determines the scope of permission.
2
Contrast with Imam Abu Hanifa's rejection of 'urf as a condition
Imam Abu Hanifa does not consider custom; he insists on an objective condition of safety, which custom cannot override.
3
Analyze the tarjih mechanism
The later fatwa prefers the Sahibayn, and Abu Hanifa himself is reported to have reverted. This shows how later Hanafi scholarship weighed custom heavily.
The reversion is reported in Ghaliyat al-Bayan.
4
Apply the principle to other fiqh cases
Consider other contracts where custom defines the expected performance, e.g., the amount of fodder for a hired animal or the type of load.
Custom differs by region and time.

Hirer's Liability When Exceeding Destination and Returning
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability after exceeding and returning in ijarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the preferred Hanafi position holds the hirer liable even after returning the animal, and distinguish it from the alternative interpretation based on contract type.

Core Idea
In ijarah, preservation is incidental to use; once use ends, returning does not restore liability protection.

This lesson examines a specific case in ijarah: a hirer takes the animal beyond the agreed destination, then returns it to the destination, after which the animal dies. The immediate ruling is liability, but the mashayikh differed on whether the ruling is absolute or depends on the contract type. We will dissect the reasoning, focusing on the nature of the obligation in ijarah versus wadi'ah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the initial ruling
The case: hire from Kufa to Hirah, exceed to Qadisiyya, return to Hirah, animal dies. The initial ruling: hirer is liable (dāmin). Note that this ruling applies to both ijarah and ariyah.
The destination Hirah is one mile from Kufa; Qadisiyya is 15 miles away.
2
Understand the first interpretation (ta'wīl)
Some mashayikh interpret the ruling restrictively: it applies only when the contract was one-way (going only). If the contract was round trip (going and returning), the hirer becomes like a depositary (mustawda') after returning, and compliance absolves liability.
This view makes the ruling dependent on the contract's scope.
3
Understand the second interpretation (absolute)
Other mashayikh hold the ruling absolute: regardless of one-way or round trip, the hirer remains liable after returning. Imam Muhammad's wording in al-Jami' al-Saghir supports this absoluteness.
This view is simpler but relies on a deeper distinction.
4
Analyse the Hanafi distinction
The key distinction: a depositary (mustawda') is commanded to preserve directly, so compliance after violation reinstates the duty. In ijarah and ariyah, preservation is commanded indirectly as a consequence of use. Once use ends (by exceeding the limit), the hirer's role as agent ends; returning does not restore it.
Thus the hirer is not like a depositary.
5
Identify the preferred view and practical consequence
The author declares the absolute view as more correct (asahh). The practical difference: under the first view, one-way contracts lead to liability after return; round trip contracts do not. Under the absolute view, liability always exists after exceeding and returning.
This affects how a mufti rules in similar cases.

قوله وقيل الجواب ... الخ - اور بعض مشائخ نے کہا ہے کہ حکم مذکور علی الاطلاق جاری ہے یعنی خواہ اس نے صرف جانے کے لئے کرایہ پر لیا ہو یا آنے اور جانے دونوں کے لئے لیا ہو۔ بہر دو صورت یہی حکم ہے کہ ضامن ہوگا۔ کیونکہ امام محمد نے جامع صغیر میں جواب کو مطلق رکھا ہے اور ودیعت و عاریت اور اجارہ کے درمیان فرق یہ ہے کہ مستودع تو بالقصد حفاظت کا مامور ہوتا ہے تو بجانب
And if he hired it to Hirah, then exceeded to Qadisiyya, then returned it to Hirah, and then it died, he is liable. And similarly for borrowed animals. And it is said that the interpretation of this case: if he hired it for going only, not for returning, so that the contract terminates upon reaching Hirah, then returning does not make him return it to the owner's hand in effect. But if he hired it for going and returning, he is like a depositary who, after violating and then returning to compliance, his duty of preservation remains, so he returns it to the owner's representative. And it is said: the answer is absolute. The difference is that the depositary is commanded to preserve directly, so the command remains after returning to compliance, so the return is to the owner's agent. In ijarah and ariyah, preservation is commanded indirectly as a consequence of use, not directly. So when use ends, he is no longer an agent, so he is not absolved by returning. And this is more correct. Translation: If he hired an animal to Hirah, then exceeded to Qadisiyya, then brought it back to Hirah, and it died, he is liable. And similarly for borrowing. It is said that the interpretation of this case: he hired it for going only, not for returning, so that the contract completes upon reaching Hirah. Then by returning, he does not return it to the owner in effect. And if he hired it for going and returning, then he is like a depositary who, after violating and then returning to compliance, his duty of preservation remains, so he returns it to the owner's agent. And it is said: the answer is absolute. The difference is that the depositary is commanded to preserve directly, so after returning to compliance, the command remains, so he returns it to the owner's agent. In ijarah and ariyah, preservation is commanded indirectly due to use, not directly. So when use ends, he is no longer an agent, so he is not absolved by returning. And this is more correct. Commentary on his statement: 'And if he hired it...' - If from Kufa to Hirah he hired or borrowed an animal, then took it beyond to Qadisiyya, then brought it back to Hirah, and the animal died, he is liable. Hirah is a city one mile from Kufa where King Nu'man bin Mundhir lived, and Qadisiyya is a place 15 miles between it and Kufa. His statement: 'It is said interpretation...' - There is a difference among the mashayikh regarding the subject of this case. The author of Hidayah states that some mashayikh interpret this case as follows: the hirer hired the animal only for going, not for returning, so that upon reaching Hirah the ijarah contract ends, and when he returns from Qadisiyya to Hirah, he does not effectively return it to the owner, meaning he remains liable. And if he hired it for both going and coming, then he is like a depositary: when he violates the order and then returns to compliance, he is absolved of liability. His statement: 'And it is said: the answer is absolute' - And some mashayikh said that the mentioned ruling applies absolutely, whether he hired it only for going or for both going and coming; in both cases the ruling is that he is liable. Because Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir left the answer absolute. The difference between wadi'ah, ariyah, and ijarah is that the depositary is commanded to preserve directly, so something...
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (among the mashayikh) regarding a specific case in ijarah. The case: a person hires an animal to go to Hirah, but instead goes beyond to Qadisiyya, then returns to Hirah, and the animal dies. The ruling: the hirer is liable. Two interpretations are discussed. First interpretation: this applies only when the contract was for one-way (going only); if it was for round trip, then after returning, the hirer is like a depositary (mustawda') who, after violating and then complying, is absolved. Second interpretation (preferred by the author): the ruling is absolute regardless of the contract type, because in ijarah and ariyah the obligation of preservation is not direct but incidental to use; once use ceases, the hirer is no longer a representative of the owner, so returning does not absolve liability. The author states that the second view is more correct (asahh). Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir left the answer absolute, supporting this view.

Key Terms
تاویل (ta'wīl): Interpretation; a reconciling explanation of a ruling to fit a particular understanding.
مودع (mūdi'): Depositary; one who receives an object for safekeeping (wadi'ah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view (asahh), why does the hirer remain liable even after returning the animal to Hirah?
A) Because he did not get the owner's consent for the return.
B) Because in ijarah, preservation is not the main purpose; once use ends, he is no longer a representative.
C) Because the animal died from exhaustion.
D) Because he should have returned it directly to the owner's home.

Key Takeaway
In ijarah, exceeding the destination ends the contractual agency, so returning does not absolve liability; the nature of preservation (incidental vs direct) distinguishes it from wadi'ah.

Ijarah — Hire and Lease

Liability When a Hired Animal Exceeds the Agreed Destination
Dispute over liability when a hired animal exceeds the limit and is returned

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on liability when a hired animal exceeds the destination and is returned, and distinguish the two views within the school.

Core Idea
Returning a hired animal after exceeding the limit does not automatically absolve liability; preservation is incidental to use.

This passage from Al-Hidayah examines a dispute among the early Hanafi mashayikh about the liability of a hirer who exceeds the agreed destination but returns the animal before it dies. Understanding this dispute clarifies the nature of the hirer's duty of preservation and how it differs from that of a depositee.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
The hirer takes a hired animal from Kufa to al-Hirah (agreed destination) but then exceeds to al-Qadisiyyah, returns to al-Hirah, and the animal dies there. Determine the contract type: one-way or round trip?
The scenario is described in the first sentence of the source.
2
State the two conflicting views
First view (ta'wil): if the contract was one-way, the hirer remains liable; if round trip, he becomes like a depositee and is absolved. Second view (absolute): the hirer is liable in all cases.
The ta'wil is attributed to some mashayikh; the absolute view is preferred by Al-Hidayah.
3
Understand the ta'wil reasoning
Under the ta'wil, a round-trip contract makes the hirer a representative of the owner for return, so returning to compliance (al-Hirah) restores him to depositee status and absolves liability.
This reasoning treats the hirer like a depositee (wadi') who violated but then returned.
4
Understand the absolute view's reasoning
In ijarah and ariyah, preservation is commanded as a consequence (taba'an) of use, not by intent (qasdan). Once use ceases (by exceeding), the hirer no longer represents the owner; returning does not restore representation.
The key distinction: wadi'ah has preservation as primary intent; ijarah/ariyah has it incidental.
5
Note the tarjih
Al-Marghinani declares the absolute view as 'asahh' (most correct), citing Imam Muhammad's unconditional answer in al-Jami' al-Saghir.
The mufta bihi position is absolute liability.

If he hires it to al-Hirah, then he exceeds with it to al-Qadisiyyah, then returns it to al-Hirah, and it dies, then he is a guarantor, and likewise in borrowing (ariyah). And it is said the interpretation of this case is if he hired it for going, not for returning, so that the contract ends upon arrival at al-Hirah, so by returning it is not returned to the owner's hand in meaning. But if he hired it for going and coming, it is like the depositee when he violates then returns to compliance. And it is said the answer is absolute, and the difference is that the depositee is commanded with preservation by intent, so the command of preservation remains after returning to compliance, so the return is achieved to the owner's representative. In hire and borrowing, preservation is commanded as a consequence of use, not by intent. So when use ceases, he is no longer a representative, so he is not absolved by returning. This is the most correct.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the liability of a hirer who takes a hired animal beyond the agreed destination (i.e., from al-Hirah to al-Qadisiyyah) and then returns it to the original destination before it perishes. The first view (with ta'wil) holds that if the contract was only for one-way travel (to al-Hirah), the hirer remains liable because the return is not part of the contract; the animal is not considered returned to the owner's control. However, if the contract included return, the hirer is like a depositee (wadi') who, after violating the contract and then returning to compliance, is absolved. The second view (absolute) says the hirer is liable in all cases because, unlike a depositee whose duty of preservation is primary, a hirer's duty is incidental to use; once use ends, he is no longer the owner's representative. Al-Hidayah declares the second view 'asahh' (most correct).

Key Terms
إجارة (ijarah): A contract of hire where one party benefits from an animal or service in exchange for payment.
عارية (ariyah): A loan of an item for use without payment, where the borrower must return the item.
ودیعة (wadi'ah): A deposit where an owner entrusts property to another for safekeeping without compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the most correct view (asahh) in the Hanafi school, when does a hirer who takes a hired animal beyond the agreed destination become free from liability if the animal dies after returning?
A) Only if the contract was for one-way travel.
B) Only if the contract was for round trip.
C) Never, because preservation is incidental to use.
D) Automatically upon returning the animal.

Key Takeaway
The hirer's duty to preserve is incidental to use; exceeding the destination makes him a guarantor, and returning does not restore his representative status.

Replacing Saddle with Packsaddle: Liability in Ijarah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for replacing a saddle with a packsaddle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on liability when a hired donkey's saddle is replaced with a packsaddle, and explain the reasoning behind Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's differing views.

Core Idea
Replacing a saddle with a packsaddle is a violation of genus, not just weight.

In ijarah (hire) contracts, the hirer must use the animal as agreed. What happens when he changes the saddle? This passage from al-Hidayah presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) on whether replacing a saddle (sarj) with a packsaddle (ikkaf) makes the hirer liable for the full value of the donkey or only for any additional weight. The key is whether the packsaddle is considered a different genus from the saddle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and agreed rules
The case: A donkey is hired with a saddle. The hirer removes the saddle and puts a different saddle or packsaddle. If the replacement saddle is of the same type as the original, there is no liability unless it is heavier. If it is not of that type, full liability. This is agreed upon by all.
The agreed rule establishes a baseline: similar items do not create liability; dissimilar items do.
2
Isolate the dispute regarding the packsaddle
The dispute concerns the packsaddle (ikkaf) that is commonly used for donkeys. The Sahibayn consider it equivalent to the saddle (same genus), so only excess weight matters. Abu Hanifa argues it is a different genus (for carrying loads, not riding), so it is a violation leading to full liability.
The packsaddle's usage and spread on the back are key distinguishing factors.
3
Analyze each proof
Sahibayn's proof: The packsaddle is commonly used, so the owner's permission implicitly includes it; only excess weight is not consented to. Abu Hanifa's proof: The packsaddle is not the same genus (saddle for riding, packsaddle for loads; different spread). It is like carrying iron instead of wheat.
Identify the illa (effective cause) in each qiyas: Sahibayn focus on common usage and consent; Abu Hanifa focuses on genus and purpose.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Under Abu Hanifa, the hirer pays the full value of the donkey. Under the Sahibayn, the hirer pays only the excess weight if heavier, otherwise nothing.
This difference affects how liability is calculated in actual legal disputes.
5
Consider tarjih (preponderance)
The text presents Abu Hanifa's view first and supports it with a strong analogy. Later Hanafi fatwa sometimes follows Abu Hanifa for strict liability, but the Sahibayn's view is also acknowledged.
No explicit tarjih in the passage; students should note that both views have weight.

Source Text

And whoever hires a donkey with a saddle, then removes that saddle and saddles it with a saddle like which donkeys are saddled, then there is no liability upon him, because when it is similar to the first, the owner's permission includes it, since there is no benefit in restricting to another, unless it exceeds in weight, then he is liable for the excess. And if it is not like which donkeys are saddled, he is liable, because the permission from his side does not include it, so he became a violator. And if he puts a packsaddle (ikkaf) like which donkeys are not packsaddled, he is liable for what we said about the saddle, and this is more appropriate. And if he puts a packsaddle like which donkeys are packsaddled, he is liable according to Abu Hanifa, and they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: he is liable for its value, because when it is like which donkeys are packsaddled, it and the saddle are equal, so the owner is pleased with it, unless it exceeds in weight, then he is liable for the excess, because he did not agree to the excess, so it becomes like the excess in the named load if it is of its kind. According to Abu Hanifa, the packsaddle is not of the same kind as the saddle, because it is for load and the saddle for riding, and similarly one spreads on the back of the animal as the other does not, so he becomes a violator, like if he carried iron while wheat was stipulated for him.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage deals with a case where a person hires a donkey with a specific saddle (sarj) and then replaces it with a different saddle or packsaddle (ikkaf). The ruling distinguishes based on similarity and usage. If the replacement saddle is of the type commonly used for donkeys and equal in weight, the hirer is not liable for the animal's death (the original permission covers it). If the replacement is not of that type, he is liable. The dispute arises when a packsaddle (ikkaf) that is commonly used for donkeys is used. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the hirer is liable for the full value because a packsaddle is not from the same genus as a saddle: the packsaddle is for carrying loads, the saddle for riding, and they spread differently on the animal's back. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that if the packsaddle is of the type commonly used, it is equivalent to a saddle, so the hirer is liable only for any excess in weight over the original saddle, because the owner would consent to a similar item. The text concludes by likening Abu Hanifa's view to a case where a person is contracted to carry wheat but carries iron instead—that is a clear violation (mukhalafah). The Sahibayn and Abu Hanifa agree that if the packsaddle is heavier, the excess weight must be compensated.

Key Terms
سرج (sarj): A saddle designed for riding (ركوب).
إكاف (ikkaf): A packsaddle used for carrying loads (حمل).
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, when does a hirer incur liability for replacing the saddle with a packsaddle that is commonly used for donkeys?
A) Always, because any replacement is a violation.
B) Only if the packsaddle is heavier than the original saddle.
C) Only if the donkey dies as a result of the packsaddle.
D) Only if the packsaddle is not commonly used.

Key Takeaway
The packsaddle's genus determines liability: Abu Hanifa views it as different (full liability), while the Sahibayn view it as equivalent (liability only for excess weight).

Liability When Replacing Saddle with Pack-Saddle in Ijara: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for replacing saddle with pack-saddle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on liability when a hired donkey's saddle is replaced with a pack-saddle, and apply the ruling to determine whether full or partial compensation is due.

Core Idea
A pack-saddle is not of the same genus as a saddle—Abu Hanifa holds full liability; Sahibayn limit it to excess value.

This passage examines a specific scenario in hire contracts: what happens when the hirer replaces the owner's saddle with a different saddle or a pack-saddle? The ruling depends on whether the replacement is of the same type (mithl) and whether it involves an excess in weight. This lesson unpacks the reasoning of Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, highlighting the practical difference in liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios
List the four cases: (1) replacing with a similar saddle, (2) replacing with a dissimilar saddle, (3) replacing with a pack-saddle of common type, (4) replacing with a pack-saddle of uncommon type.
2
Extract the ruling for each scenario
State the liability (no liability, liability for excess, or full liability) for each case from the passage.
3
Focus on the disputed case
Isolate the case where a common pack-saddle replaces the saddle. Note the difference: Abu Hanifa says full liability; Sahibayn say only for excess.
This is the core intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Analyze the proofs
For Abu Hanifa: the pack-saddle is a different genus (jins) due to different purpose and spread. For Sahibayn: if commonly used, it is effectively the same (mithl) so permission covers it except for excess.
Understand the distinction between mithl and jins.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
The practical consequence: full vs. partial liability. The text notes that some later authorities (e.g., Khawaharzada) consider the full liability report as asahh, but both views are transmitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن اكترى حمارا بسرج فنزع ذالک السرج و اسرجه بسرج يُسرج بمثله الحمر فلا ضمان عليه لانه اذا كان يماثل الاول يتناوله اذن المالك اذ لا فائدة فى التقييد بغيره الا اذا كان زائدا عليه في الوزن فحينئذ يضمن الزيادة وان كان لا يسرج بمثله الحمر يضمن لأنه لم يتناوله الاذن من جهته فصار مخالفا وان اوكفه ياكاف لا يوكف بمثله الحمر يضمن لما قلنا فى السرج وهذا اولى وان او كفه با كاف يوكف بمثله الحمر ضمن عند ابي حنيفة وقالا يضمن بحسابه لانه اذا كان يوكف بمثله الحمر كان هو والسرج سواء فيكون المالک راضيا به الا اذا كان زائدا على السرج فى الوزن فيضمن الزيادة لانه لم يرض بالزيادة فصار كالزيادة في الحمل المسمى اذا كانت من جنسه ولابي حنيفة ان الاكاف ليس من جنس السرج لانه للحمل والسرج للركوب وكذا ينبسط احدهما على ظهر الدابة ما لا ينبسط عليه الآخر فيكون مخالفا كما اذا حمل الحديد وقد شرط له الحنطة
Whoever hires a donkey with a saddle, then removes that saddle and saddles it with a saddle with which donkeys are similarly saddled, there is no liability upon him, because when it resembles the first, the owner's permission includes it, since there is no benefit in restricting to something else, unless it is heavier in weight, then he is liable for the excess. And if it is not a saddle with which donkeys are similarly saddled, he is liable because the owner's permission does not include it, so he has acted contrary. And if he places a pack-saddle that is not such as is placed on donkeys, he is liable for what we said regarding the saddle, and this is more fitting. And if he places a pack-saddle that is such as is placed on donkeys, he is liable according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: he is liable proportionate to it, because when it is such as is placed on donkeys, the pack-saddle and the saddle are equal, so the owner is satisfied with it, unless it is heavier in weight than the saddle, then he is liable for the excess because the owner is not satisfied with the excess, so it is like the excess in the named load when it is of its type. And for Abu Hanifa: the pack-saddle is not of the type of saddle, because it is for loading and the saddle for riding, and likewise one spreads on the back of the animal what the other does not, so he is acting contrary, just as if he carried iron when wheat was stipulated for him.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding liability when a hired donkey's saddle is replaced by the hirer. The rule begins with the case where the hirer replaces the original saddle with a similar saddle: there is no liability because the owner's implicit permission covers any saddle of the same type, provided it is not heavier. If the replacement saddle is of a type not normally used for donkeys, the hirer is liable for the full value. The discussion then turns to replacing the saddle with a pack-saddle (palān). If the pack-saddle is not of the type commonly used for donkeys, liability is clear. If it is of a common type, Imam Abu Hanifa holds the hirer fully liable, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say liability is only for the excess value or weight. The rationale for Abu Hanifa is that the pack-saddle is fundamentally different in purpose (load-bearing vs. riding) and spread on the animal's back, so it is a complete change of item, not merely an excess. The Sahibayn analogize it to the saddle, considering it as a replacement of equal type, so only the excess portion beyond the original saddle's value/weight is liable.

Key Terms
سرج (saraj): The saddle placed on a donkey or horse for riding.
إكاف (ikāf): A pack-saddle used for loading burdens on an animal, distinct from a riding saddle.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability to compensate for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No liability
B) Liability only for the excess weight
C) Full liability
D) Liability for half the value

Key Takeaway
The liability for replacing a saddle with a pack-saddle in ijara depends on whether the pack-saddle is considered of the same genus as the saddle; Abu Hanifa says full liability, Sahibayn limit to excess.

Liability of Porter Taking Customary Route
Ruling on liability when a hired porter takes a different route that is commonly used

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a hired porter takes a customary alternative route and identify the role of custom in determining liability.

Core Idea
A hired porter who takes a commonly used alternative route is not liable for loss — custom validates the deviation.

In hire contracts, the specified route is usually binding. However, Hanafi fiqh recognizes that if an alternative route is equally customary, the porter's deviation is not a breach. This lesson examines the ruling and the underlying principle of custom ('urf) in ijara.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the porter taking a different route from the one specified. The ruling is that the porter is not liable for any loss of the goods.
Liability is negated even though the contract specified a route.
2
Locate the legal basis
The basis is the principle that custom ('urf) is a valid consideration in contracts. Since the alternative route is commonly used, it is not a breach.
Custom validates the deviation.
3
Understand the condition for exemption
The alternative route must be customary (i.e., people commonly take it). If the route is unusual or dangerous, liability may attach.
The condition is 'commonly used by people' (يسلكه الناس).
4
Apply the principle to new scenarios
Consider a delivery driver who takes a common shortcut instead of the specified highway. The same ruling applies: no liability if the shortcut is customary.
Custom can change over time; what is customary may vary by location.

Source Text

And if he hires a porter to carry food for him via a certain route, and he (the porter) takes another route that people take, and the goods perish, then (there is no liability?)

This passage states a ruling about a hired porter who deviates from the specified route but takes an alternative route that is commonly used by people. The ruling, though incomplete in the Arabic text, is that the porter is not liable for the loss. The reason is that the route he took is also customary, so the deviation does not constitute a violation of the contract that would trigger liability. The Urdu commentary clarifies that if the goods perish, the porter is not liable. This is based on the principle that custom ('urf) is given weight in determining what is acceptable in hire contracts, as long as the alternative route is not clearly detrimental.

Key Terms

Liability for Changing Saddle Type: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for saddle change

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the basis of the intra-Hanafi dispute on saddle change liability and apply the differing views to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Changing the genus of a saddle triggers liability for the whole; changing only quantity triggers liability for the excess.

In a hire contract (ijāra), the hirer must use the animal as agreed. What happens if he changes the saddle type? The Hanafi masters differ on whether he is liable for the full value or only the excess. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind Imam Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions, focusing on the distinction between a change of genus versus a change in quantity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
What is the dispute? The hirer removed the original riding saddle (zayn) and replaced it with a pack-saddle (pallān). Is he liable for the whole animal value or only the excess?
The liability arises if the animal perishes; the contract terms were violated.
2
Analyze Sahibayn's position
They hold liability limited to the excess (qadr al-zāʾid) measured by area or weight. Their proof: if the pallān is a common type, it is akin to adding extra load of the same kind.
Excess is measured proportionally: e.g., if pallān covers twice the area, liable for half the value.
3
Analyze Imam Abu Hanifa's position
He holds liability for the whole value (or at least the whole, depending on narration). His proof: the pallān is a different genus (jins) from the zayn – one for burden, one for riding – so it is a completely different item, not permitted by the owner.
The difference in function and covering area makes it a distinct item.
4
Evaluate the narrations from Abu Hanifa
There are two conflicting reports: in al-Jāmiʿ al-Ṣaghīr, he is liable for the whole; in al-Ijārāt, only for the excess. Shaykh al-Islam Khwāhar Zādah preferred the first as aṣaḥ (more authentic).
This affects the fatwa today – which narration is relied upon.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa's preferred view, the hirer may owe the full replacement cost; under Sahibayn, only a fraction. The practical difference is significant in complex cases.
The dispute is over the extent of liability, not the fact of liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قوله وقالا يضمن بحسابه ... الخ - صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ زیادتی کے حساب سے ضامن ہوگا۔ اب یہ زیاتی پیمائش کے لحاظ سے مراد ہے یاوزن کے لحاظ سے؟ بعض مشائخ نے کہا ہے کہ پیمائش کے حساب سے مراد ہے کہ اگر زین چوپایہ کی پیٹھ سے دو بالشت او پالان چار بالشت جگہ گھیر تا ہو تو نصف قیمت کا ضامن ہوگا۔ اور بعض مشائخ نے کہا ہے کہ وزن اور نقل و خفت کے حساب سے مراد ہے

یہاں تک کہ اگر زین کا وزن دومن اور پالان کا وزن چھ من ہو تو قیمت کے دونلث کا ضامن ہوگا۔ قوله لانه اذا كان .... الخ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ جب وہ پالان ایسا ہے جیسا گدھوں پر لگایا جاتا ہے تو پالان اور زین دونوں برابر ہو گئے تو مالک اس پر بھی راضی ہوگا اور پالان کے زمین سے زیادہ وزنی ہونے کی صورت میں مالک اس زیادتی سے راضی نہیں تو ایسا ہو گیا جیسے بوجھ کی مقدار بیان کر دی گئی پھر اسی جنس کا بوجھ اس سے زائد لاد دیا کہ اگر جانور تلف ہو جائے تو بقدر زیادتی ضامن ہوتا ہے۔

قوله ولابي حنيفة ... الخ - امام ابو حنيفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ پالان از جنس زمین نہیں ہے کیونکہ پالان بار برداری کے لئے اور زین سواری کے لئے ہوتی ہے تو غیر جنس کی وجہ سے ضامن ہوگا۔ نیز جانور کی پیٹھ پر پالان کا پھیلا ؤزین سے زیادہ ہوتا ہے۔
[Text as above: Urdu commentary on Hidayah matn. Arabic matn: 'And if he put a saddle that is not normally put on donkeys, he is liable...' Sahibayn say: 'He is liable according to the excess.' Abu Hanifa's evidence: the saddle is not of the same type as the original.]
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute (ikhtilaf) between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Sahibayn: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding a hired donkey or animal. The hirer removed the original saddle (zeen) and replaced it with a different type (pallan). If the pallan is of a kind not normally used for donkeys, the hirer is liable for the entire value of the animal if it perishes, because the owner's permission did not extend to such a change. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that even if the pallan is of a commonly used type, the hirer is liable because the pallan is a different genus (jins) from the zeen (one is for burden, the other for riding) and covers more area on the animal's back. The Sahibayn differ: they say the hirer is liable only for the excess (zāʾid), measured either by area or weight. For example, if the pallan covers twice the area or weighs three times as much, the hirer is liable for half or two-thirds of the value respectively. There are conflicting narrations from Abu Hanifa: one (in al-Jāmiʿ al-Ṣaghīr) says he is liable for the whole value; another (in al-Ijārāt) says only for the excess. Shaykh al-Islam Khwāhar Zādah preferred the first. The dispute is over the extent of liability, not the fact of liability.

Key Terms
زین (zayn): A saddle for riding (used on horses/donkeys for riding).
پالان (pallān): A pack-saddle for carrying loads on donkeys.
ضامن (ḍāmin): Liable (for compensation or full value).
قدر زائد (qadr al-zāʾid): The excess (in area or weight) over the original.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the dispute over changing a saddle on a hired donkey, what is the basis for Imam Abu Hanifa's view that the hirer is liable for the whole value?
A) The pallān is heavier than the original saddle.
B) The pallān is of a different genus (jins) from the original saddle.
C) The owner explicitly forbade any change.
D) The hirer did not return the animal on time.

Key Takeaway
The key takeaway is that in Hanafi fiqh, a change of genus (jins) in a hired animal's equipment leads to full liability, while a change within the same genus leads to proportional liability for the excess.

Hired Porter Taking an Alternate Route: Liability and Wages
Ruling on liability when a hired porter deviates from a stipulated route

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of beneficial condition (taqyīd mufīd) to determine liability and wages when a hired porter deviates from the specified route.

Core Idea
If an alternate route is equivalent and commonly used, the stipulation is non-beneficial and the porter is not liable.

In hire contracts (ijāra), the employer may stipulate a specific path for the porter. If the porter takes a different route, we must assess whether the condition was beneficial (mufīd) or not. This lesson unpacks the ruling from Al-Hidāyah, showing that liability only arises when the deviation introduces a material difference (tafāwut) that undermines the employer's benefit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stipulation and the deviation
State the agreed path and the alternate route taken by the porter. Note whether the alternate route is commonly used by people.
The text mentions two scenarios: a public road and an uncommon path.
2
Assess material difference (tafāwut)
Compare the two routes in terms of safety, distance, and risk. If they are essentially the same, there is no tafāwut; if significantly different (e.g., land vs. sea), there is gross tafāwut.
Hanafi: tafāwut fāḥish triggers liability; minor differences are ignored.
3
Apply the principle of beneficial condition (taqyīd mufīd)
If the alternate route is equivalent, the condition is non-beneficial (ghayr mufīd), so deviation does not violate the contract. If the route differs materially, the condition is beneficial and deviation is a breach.
This principle is derived from qiyās: a condition is binding only if it serves a purpose.
4
Determine liability for loss
If the condition was non-beneficial and the goods perished, no liability (ḍamān). If beneficial and goods perish, porter is liable. For sea vs. land, always liable due to gross difference.
Liability is for the value of the goods at the time of loss.
5
Determine wages if delivery is completed
Even if the porter deviated, if he ultimately delivers the goods, he is entitled to full wages. The breach is considered resolved in meaning (maʿnan) though it remains in form (ṣūratan).
This prevents unjust enrichment of the employer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان استاجر حمّالا ليحمل له طعاما فى طريق كذا فاخذ فى طريق غيره يسلكه الناس فهلك المتاع فلا ضمان عليه وان بلغ فله الاجر وهذا اذا لم يكن بين الطريقين تفاوت لان عند ذالك التقييد غير مفيد اما اذا كان تفاوت يضمن لصحة التقييد فانه تقييد مفيد الا ان الظاهر عدم التفاوت اذا كان طريقا يسلكه الناس فلم يفصل وان كان طريقا لا يسلكه الناس فهلك ضمن لانه صح التقييد فصار مخالفا وان بلغ فله الاجر لانه ارتفع الخلاف معنى وان بقى صورة وان حمله فى البحر فيما يحمله الناس في البر ضمن لفحش التفاوت بين البر والبحر وان بلغ فله الاجر لحصول المقصود وارتفاع الخلاف معنى

توضیح اللغة جمال بوجھ اٹھانے والا ، مزدور، قلی، مسلکہ (ن) سلو کا راستہ چلنا، متاع چاندی سونے کے علاوہ سامان زندگی۔ مبلغ تبلیغاً پہنچا دینا۔ برخشک زمین ، بیابان، انتشار پھیلنا۔ عروق جمع عرق رگ۔ ترجمہ اگر اجارہ پر لیا جمال کو تا کہ اٹھائے اس کا اناج فلاں راہ سے فلاں مقام تک ۔ پس جمال نے اس کے سوا دوسرا راستہ اختیار کیا جس میں لوگ چلتے ہیں۔ پس سامان ضائع ہو گیا تو اس پر ضمان نہیں اور اگر اس نے پہنچا دیا تو اس کے لئے مزدوری ہوگی ۔ یہ اس وقت ہے جب دونوں راستوں میں تفاوت نہ ہو ۔ کیونکہ اس صورت میں قید لگانا غیر مفید ہے۔ اور اگر تفاوت ہو تو ضامن ہوگا۔ صحت تنقید کی وجہ سے۔ کیونکہ یہ قید لگانا مفید ہے۔ لیکن ظاہر عدم تفاوت ہے۔ جب کہ وہ راستہ ایسا ہے کہ لوگ اس میں چلتے ہیں۔ اس لئے حکم میں تفصیل نہیں کی ۔ اور اگر راستہ ایسا ہو کہ لوگ اس میں نہیں چلتے اور سامان ضائع ہو جائے تو ضامن ہوگا۔ کیونکہ قید لگا نا صحیح ہوا تو جمال مخالف ہو گیا اور اگر اس نے پہنچا دیا تو اس کے لئے اجرت ہوگی ۔ کیونکہ از راہ معنی اختلاف اٹھ گیا اگر چہ صورۃ باقی ہے اور اگر اس کو دریا میں لا دلایا جس کو لوگ خشکی میں لاتے ہیں تو ضامن ہوگا۔ خشکی و تری میں تفاوت فاحش ہونے کی وجہ سے اور اگر اس نے پہنچا دیا تو اس کے لئے اجرت ہو گی مقصود حاصل ہو جانے اور ازارہ معنی خلاف اٹھ جانے کی وجہ سے ۔

تشریح قوله وان استاجر حمالة ... الخ کسی نے ایک حمال اس لئے اجارہ پر لیا تا کہ وہ یہ اناج فلاں راہ سے فلاں مقام وہ یہ تک پہنچا دے۔ جمال نے اس طے کردہ راہ کے علاوہ دوسرا راستہ اختیار کیا کہ اس میں بھی لوگوں کی آمد ورفت رہتی ہے اور حال یہ کہ دونوں راستوں میں کوئی تفاوت نہیں ہے۔ پھر بھی اناج ضائع ہو گیا تو جمال پر کوئی ضمان نہ ہو گا۔ اور اگر اس نے اناج پہنچا دیا تو وہ مزدوری کا مستحق ہوگا۔ کیونکہ تفاوت نہ ہونے کی صورت میں کسی خاص راستہ کی قید لگانا غیر مفید ہے۔ اور اگر دونوں راستوں میں تفاوت ہو یا جس ماہ سے وہ گیا ہے اس میں لوگوں کی آمد ورفت نہ ہو تو نہ امن ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس صورت میں خاص راستہ کی قید لگانا مفید اور سیح ہے تو جمال نے
And if he hired a porter to carry his grain on a specific path, and he took another path which people use, and the goods perished, then no liability on him. If he delivered, he gets wages. This is when there is no difference between the two paths. If there is difference, he is liable. If he carries by sea what people carry by land, he is liable due to vast difference...
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling for a hired porter (ḥammāl) who deviates from the stipulated route. The key factor is whether the alternative route is commonly used and whether it differs significantly from the agreed route. If the alternative is a usual route with no significant difference (in safety, distance, etc.), the stipulation of a specific path is considered non-beneficial (ghayr mufīd), so the porter is not liable for loss but still entitled to wages if the goods are delivered. If the alternative route differs (e.g., riskier, longer, or not commonly used), the stipulation is beneficial, and the porter is liable for loss. However, if he delivers despite the deviation, he gets wages because the purpose is achieved and the breach is effectively resolved. A special case: if he transports by sea what is normally carried by land, the difference is gross (fāḥish), so he is liable even if he delivers? Actually the text says he is liable if loss occurs, but if he delivers, he gets wages. The rationale is that the sea route is vastly different from land, so the condition is meaningful. The Urdu commentary elaborates on these points, stressing that the default is no difference if the alternative is a public road.

Key Terms
تقييد (taqyīd): The condition/stipulation of a specific path in the hire contract.
مفيد (mufīd): Beneficial; a condition that makes a meaningful difference.
تفاوت (tafāwut): Significant difference (in travel conditions, safety, etc.) between routes.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability to compensate for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A hired porter takes an alternate route that is also commonly used and no different from the agreed path. The goods are lost. What is the ruling?
A) The porter is liable for the full value.
B) The porter is not liable but gets no wages.
C) The porter is not liable and gets wages if he delivers.
D) The porter is liable only for the difference in distance.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a contractual condition is only binding if it is beneficial; deviation from a non-beneficial condition does not incur liability.

Hiring Land for Wheat But Planting Raṭba: Liability and Wages
Ruling on liability when hirer plants a different crop than agreed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability and wages when a hirer plants a different, more harmful crop, and explain the reasoning by analogy to a usurper.

Core Idea
Planting a more harmful crop voids the contract and makes the hirer a usurper, liable for damage and no wage.

Contracts of hire (ijāra) require strict adherence to terms. When a hirer uses the land for a more harmful purpose than agreed, the law treats him as a trespasser. This lesson examines the ruling from al-Hidāyah when wheat is replaced by raṭba, a damaging fodder crop.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the contract breach
The hirer agreed to plant wheat (ḥinṭa) but planted raṭba, a more harmful crop. This is a change for the worse (khilāf ilā sharr).
Raṭba has spreading roots and high water needs, damaging the land more than wheat.
2
Determine the legal consequences
The hirer is liable for the decrease in land value (naqṣ) and is not entitled to any wage (ujra).
These are two separate consequences: compensation and forfeiture.
3
Locate the proof (dalīl)
The ruling is based on qiyas (analogy) to a usurper (ghāṣib). The hirer's unauthorized harmful use makes him similar to one who uses land without any right.
The proof is not from a direct text but from shared ʿilla (effective cause).
4
Trace the ʿilla (effective cause)
The ʿilla is the unauthorized harmful use: raṭba is more harmful than wheat due to its roots and water needs. Therefore, the hirer's use is akin to usurpation.
If the change were harmless or beneficial, the ruling might differ.
5
Apply the ruling to similar cases
Any change to a crop that is equally or less harmful does not trigger this strict liability; wages remain due.
The key is the degree of harm relative to the agreed crop.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن استاجر ارضا ليزرعها حنطة فزرعها رطبة ضمن ما نقصها لان الرطاب اضر بالارض من الحنطة لانتشار عروقها فيها وكثرة الحاجة الى سقيها فكان خلافا الى شر فيضمن ما نقصها ولا اجر له لانه غاصب للارض على ما قررناه
And whoever hires land to plant wheat then plants raṭba (a type of clover/grass) is liable for the damage it causes because raṭab plants are more harmful to the land than wheat due to the spread of their roots and greater need for watering, so it is a change for the worse; he is liable for the damage and has no wage because he is a usurper (ghāṣib) of the land as we have established.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage gives a ruling from the Hidayah on hiring agricultural land. The hirer (mustaʾjir) contracts to plant wheat (ḥinṭa) but instead plants raṭba (a succulent fodder crop). Since raṭba has spreading roots and requires more frequent watering, it damages the land more than wheat would. Therefore, the hirer is considered to have violated the contract in a way that harms the owner's property. He is liable for the decrease in the land's value (mā naqaṣahā). Additionally, he is not entitled to any wage (ujra) for his use of the land because he is effectively a usurper (ghāṣib) – his use was without valid permission since he did not fulfill the contract terms. The commentary 'as we have established' refers to the earlier principle that a person who uses property without proper authorization is a ghāṣib and must pay compensation but gets no benefit.

Key Terms
رطبة (raṭba): A green fodder crop (clover/alfalfa) that damages land due to extensive root system and high water need.
حنطة (ḥinṭa): Wheat; the crop specified in the hire contract.
غاصب (ghāṣib): Usurper; one who uses property without right, liable for damage and not entitled to profit.
نقص (naqṣ): Decrease in value or quality of the land due to the crop planted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A farmer hires land to plant wheat but plants raṭba instead. The raṭba damages the land. What are the consequences according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) The farmer must pay the agreed wage but is not liable for damage.
B) The farmer is liable for the damage and is not entitled to any wage.
C) The farmer must plow under the raṭba and plant wheat without extra cost.
D) The farmer is not liable if the land is not permanently damaged.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a hirer who deviates from the contract in a harmful way becomes a usurper, liable for damage and forfeiting wages.

Ruling on the hired porter who takes a different route: useful vs useless stipulations
Ruling on porter's liability for taking an alternative route

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a hired porter's liability when he takes a different route and identify the criterion of 'significant difference' (tafāwut) that renders a stipulation beneficial.

Core Idea
A useful stipulation binds; a useless one is ignored – liability hinges on the route's significance.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a common scenario in hire contracts (ijāra): the hired porter (hammāl) is instructed to take a specific route but takes another. The ruling distinguishes between significant and insignificant differences in routes, based on whether the stipulation was beneficial. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining liability for loss and entitlement to the wage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stipulation
The contract includes a condition (taqyīd) specifying the route. First, confirm the existence of this condition.
The stipulation is valid if it is beneficial (mufīd).
2
Assess the alternative route's difference
Compare the stipulated route and the actual route: is there a significant difference (tafāwut) in safety, distance, or usage? The text considers roads people commonly travel as similar by default.
3
Apply the rule for loss
If the routes are similar (no tafāwut), the stipulation is useless (ghayr mufīd), so no liability (daman) for loss. If they differ significantly, the stipulation is beneficial, and the porter is liable if goods are lost.
4
Apply the rule for delivery
Regardless of liability for loss, if the porter delivers the goods, he is entitled to the wage because the objective (maqsūd) is achieved, and the contravention is removed in meaning.
This applies even in cases of gross difference like sea vs land.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان بلغ فله الاجر وهذا اذا لم يكن بين الطريقين تفاوت لان عند ذالك التقييد غير مفيد اما اذا كان تفاوت يضمن لصحة التقييد فانه تقييد مفيد الا ان الظاهر عدم التفاوت اذا كان طريقا يسلكه الناس فلم يفصل وان كان طريقا لا يسلكه الناس فهلك ضمن لانه صح التقييد فصار مخالفا وان بلغ فله الاجر لانه ارتفع الخلاف معنى وان بقى صورة وان حمله فى البحر فيما يحمله الناس في البر ضمن لفحش التفاوت بين البر والبحر وان بلغ فله الاجر لحصول المقصود وارتفاع الخلاف معنى
And if he delivers, he has the wage. This is when there is no difference between the two routes, because at that time the stipulation is useless. But if there is a difference, he is liable due to the validity of the stipulation, because it is a useful stipulation. However, the apparent assumption is no difference if it is a road that people travel, so he is not distinguished. And if it is a road that people do not travel and the goods are lost, he is liable because the stipulation is valid and he has contravened. And if he delivers, he has the wage because the contravention is removed in meaning, even if it remains in form. And if he transports it by sea while people transport it by land, he is liable due to the gross difference between land and sea. And if he delivers, he has the wage because the objective is achieved and the contravention is removed in meaning.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage states the ruling for a hired porter (hammal) who is hired to carry goods via a specific route but takes a different route. The key distinction is whether the two routes differ significantly. If there is no significant difference—meaning the alternative route is also commonly used by people and similar in safety and distance—then the stipulation of the specific route is considered non-beneficial (ghayr mufid). In that case, if the goods are lost, the porter is not liable (no daman), because he did not truly contravene the contract. If he delivers, he is entitled to the agreed wage. If, however, the routes are significantly different—either in terms of danger, distance, or lack of public traffic—then the stipulation becomes meaningful. If the goods are lost, the porter is liable because he contravened the contract. But if he delivers despite the different route, he receives the wage because the ultimate objective (delivery) is met, and the contravention is considered removed in meaning. The same applies if he takes a sea route when the contract was for land transport: due to the gross disparity, he is liable for loss but still entitled to wage if he delivers. This ruling is based on the principle that a useful stipulation must be honored; if it is not useful, it is disregarded.

Key Terms
حمال (hammāl): a porter or carrier hired to transport goods
تقييد (taqyīd): stipulation or condition specifying a particular route or method in the hire contract
تفاوت (tafāwut): significant difference or disparity between two options (e.g., routes) in terms of safety, distance, or usage

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A porter is hired to take goods via a road that is not commonly used (a dangerous route). He takes a commonly used safe road instead, and the goods are lost. According to the text, what is the ruling?
A) The porter is liable because he contravened the stipulation
B) The porter is not liable because the alternative route is better and the stipulation was meaningless
C) The porter is liable only if he was negligent
D) The porter is not liable because the goods were lost on a public road

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a stipulation must be beneficial to be binding; otherwise, deviation does not incur liability, but delivery always entitles the wage.

Ruling on Hiring Land for Wheat but Planting Clover
Ruling when a land tenant plants clover instead of wheat as stipulated

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling when a tenant substitutes a more harmful crop than specified and determine that the tenant becomes a usurper with liability and no rent.

Core Idea
Planting a more harmful crop than stipulated makes the tenant a usurper—liable for damage and not entitled to rent.

Imagine you hire out your land for wheat cultivation. The tenant decides to plant clover instead, which damages the soil more. How does Hanafi law treat such a breach? This ruling from Al-Hidayah clarifies that the tenant becomes a usurper because of the harmful deviation, losing any claim to rent and becoming liable for the land's decreased value. Understanding this principle helps you see how Hanafi fiqh balances contractual freedom with protection of property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contractual condition
The tenant was hired to plant wheat. This is a specific condition (shart) in the ijarah contract.
2
Evaluate the substitute crop
Clover (ratbah) is more harmful than wheat due to spreading roots and high water needs. This makes the substitution a contravention towards evil (sharr).
The harmful nature is key.
3
Apply the principle of harmful contravention
Because the tenant deviated to a more harmful use, his possession becomes wrongful, making him a ghāṣib (usurper).
This overrides the original consent.
4
Determine the consequences
As a usurper, he is liable for the decrease in land value (damān mā naqaṣa) and receives no rent.
Contrast: if the substitute were less harmful, the ruling might differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن استاجر ارضا ليزرعها حنطة فزرعها رطبة ضمن ما نقصها لان الرطاب اضر بالارض من الحنطة لانتشار عروقها فيها وكثرة الحاجة الى سقيها فكان خلافا الى شر فيضمن ما نقصها ولا اجر له لانه غاصب للارض على ما قررناه
And whoever hires land to plant it with wheat and plants it with clover (ratbah), he is liable for what it diminished [in value], because clover is more harmful to the land than wheat due to the spread of its roots in it and the greater need for watering it. So it is a contravention towards evil, so he is liable for what it diminished, and he has no wage because he is a usurper of the land according to what we have established.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where a person hires land with the specific condition to plant wheat (hanṭah), but instead plants clover (ratbah). Clover is a perennial plant with spreading roots and higher water needs, which damages the land more than wheat. Since the tenant violated the contract by planting something more harmful, he is considered a usurper (ghāṣib) of the land. Consequently, he must pay compensation for the decrease in the land's value (damān mā naqaṣahā). Additionally, he is not entitled to any rental payment because his possession was wrongful; a usurper does not owe rent but is liable for damages. The ruling emphasizes that the contravention was towards evil (sharr) rather than good, hence full liability.

Key Terms
رطبة (raṭbah): clover or alfalfa, a perennial plant with spreading roots and high water needs
غاصب (ghāṣib): usurper; one who takes property without right, here because the tenant used the land for a purpose not agreed upon
ضمن ما نقصها (ḍamina mā naqaṣahā): liable for the decrease in value caused to the land

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the tenant who plants clover instead of wheat become a usurper (ghāṣib) and not simply owe the rent?
A) Because clover is more profitable than wheat
B) Because he contravened the contract by planting something more harmful, so his use was wrongful
C) Because he failed to water the land
D) Because the landowner did not consent

Key Takeaway
A tenant who substitutes a more harmful crop than stipulated becomes a usurper, liable for damage and not entitled to rent.

Customer's Choice When Tailor Sews a Different Garment
Ruling with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on customer's choice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on breach of hire contract for garment manufacturing, distinguishing between the zāhir position and Abu Hanifa's view, and state the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
When a tailor sews a different garment, the customer chooses: accept with customary wage or claim cloth value.

Imagine you give cloth to a tailor to sew a shirt for a dirham, but he makes a cloak instead. Are you forced to pay for the cloak, or can you demand the cloth's value? The Hanafi school debates this. The relied-upon view grants you a choice: either keep the cloak and pay a fair wage, or hold the tailor liable for the cloth. This lesson unpacks the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and legal issue
Identify the hire contract (ijara) for sewing a shirt. The tailor makes a cloak instead. The key question: does the customer have a choice (khiyār) or is the tailor automatically liable?
The cloth is the principal's property; the tailor is an agent.
2
Identify the two Hanafi positions
The zāhir (apparent) position holds the customer has a choice. Abu Hanifa (within the school) holds the tailor is automatically liable without choice. Record both.
The text uses 'zāhir' for the first and 'an Abi Hanifa' for the second.
3
Analyze the proofs for each
The zāhir view argues the cloak and shirt share sufficient benefit (both tied at waist, worn similarly) so partial compliance exists. Abu Hanifa argues they are different genera (jins), thus complete contravention. Note the talili reasoning.
An analogy with beating a basin vs. a cup is used to support choice.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Under the zāhir view, the customer can either claim the cloth's value (daman) or take the cloak and pay ajr al-mithl (≤ named wage). Under Abu Hanifa, only liability for cloth value without option.
The difference affects whether the tailor's work is compensated if accepted.
5
Identify the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position
The text concludes 'al-asahh annahu yukhayyar' – the more correct view is that the customer has a choice. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
This resolves the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن دفع الى خياط ثوبا ليخيط قميصا بدرهم فخاطه قباء فان شاء ضمنه قيمة الثوب وان شاء اخذ القباء واعطاه اجر مثله ولا يجاوز به درهما قيل معناه القرطق الذى هو ذو طاق واحد لانه يستعمل استعمال القباء وقيل هو مجرى على اطلاقه لانهما يتقاربان فى المنفعة وعن ابي حنيفة انه يضمن من غير خيار ولان القباء خلاف جنس القميص ووجه الظاهر انه قميص من وجه لانه يشد وسطه وينتفع به انتفاع القميص فجاءت الموافقة والمخالفة فيميل الى اى الجهتين شاء الا انه يجب اجر المثل لقصور جهة الموافقة ولا يجاوز به الدرهم المسمى كما هو الحكم فى سائر الاجارات الفاسدة على ما نبينه فى بابه ان شاء الله تعالى ولو سراويل وقد امر بالقباء قيل يضمن من غير خيار للتفاوت فى المنفعة والاصح انه يخير للاتحاد في صل المنفعة وصار كما اذا امر بضرب طست من شبه فضرب منه كوزا فانه يخير كذا هذا
And whoever gives cloth to a tailor to sew a shirt for a dirham and he sews a cloak, then if he wishes, he makes him liable for the value of the cloth, and if he wishes, he takes the cloak and gives him its similar wage and does not exceed the named dirham. It is said its meaning is the qurṭaq which is single-layered because it is used like a cloak. And it is said it is taken in its absolute sense because they are similar in benefit. And from Abu Hanifa that he makes him liable without choice, because the cloak is a different type from the shirt. And the apparent view is that it is a shirt in one sense because its middle is tied and one benefits from it like a shirt; thus concord and difference exist, so he inclines to whichever side he wishes, except that the similar wage is obligatory due to weakness of the concord aspect, and it does not exceed the named dirham as is the ruling in all invalid hire contracts, as we will explain in its chapter, God willing. And if [he ordered] trousers while he was ordered to make a cloak, it is said he makes him liable without choice due to the difference in benefit, and the more correct view is that he has a choice due to unity in the root of benefit, and it becomes like the case where he ordered to beat a basin of brass and he beat a cup from it, for he has a choice—likewise this.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage discusses the case where a customer gives cloth to a tailor, ordering a shirt (qamīṣ) for a specified wage of one dirham. The tailor instead sews a cloak (qabā'). The ruling gives the customer an option: either demand compensation for the value of the cloth (daman), or accept the cloak and pay the tailor the customary wage (ajr al-mithl) for that work, not exceeding the agreed dirham. The text then presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: according to Abu Hanifa, the tailor is liable without choice (yadmanu min ghayri khiyār) because a cloak is a completely different type from a shirt. However, the more apparent (zāhir) position is that the customer has a choice, because the cloak is like a shirt in some respects (e.g., it is tied at the waist and worn similarly). The customer can lean towards either aspect (conciliation or contravention). But since the contravention exists, the tailor receives the customary wage (not exceeding the named price) because the work was beneficial. The passage also mentions that if the tailor makes trousers (sirwāl) instead of a cloak, the same ikhtilaf applies: some say the tailor is liable without choice due to different benefit; the more correct position is that the customer has a choice because both garments serve a similar purpose (protection and covering). An analogy is drawn with a metalworker: if ordered to make a basin (ṭast) from brass but makes a cup (kūz), the customer has a choice. This shows the Hanafi principle that when the service is beneficial but contravenes the order, the customer may either reject or accept with compensation.

Key Terms
قباء (qabā'): a cloak or outer garment worn over other clothes, often open in front
أجر المثل (ajr al-mithl): the customary or fair wage for similar work
الإجارة الفاسدة (al-ijārah al-fāsidah): a voidable or invalid hire contract, where the service is performed but the terms are defective

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (asahh) view in the Hanafi school, if a tailor makes a cloak instead of a shirt, what is the customer's right?
A) The customer must pay the full agreed wage
B) The customer can only claim the cloth's value from the tailor
C) The customer may either claim the cloth's value or accept the cloak and pay the customary wage
D) The customer must accept the cloak and pay nothing

Key Takeaway
In a fāsid hire contract where the worker performs a different but functionally similar task, the employer has a choice between compensation and acceptance with customary wage.
